SUBJECT:
Regional Measure 3 Funding Request — Approval
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to authorize the City Manager to execute and submit an allocation request as required by the 
Metropolitan Transportation Commission (MTC) for $3 million in Regional Measure 3 (RM3) funds for 19th Avenue/Fashion 
Island Boulevard Multimodal Improvements Project and to designate the City of San Mateo as the implementation agency 
for the construction phase. 
BACKGROUND:
The 19th Avenue and Fashion Island Boulevard Multimodal Improvements Project will deliver targeted congestion relief 
and safety enhancements along the corridor surrounding 19th Avenue and Fashion Island Boulevard in San Mateo. Key 
features include construction of a Class IV separated bikeway (protected, two-way bike lane) along the full corridor, 
connecting the Hayward Park Caltrain Station to the future SamTrans Mobility Hub at the existing Caltrans Park and Ride 
lot, as well as optimized signal operations to improve traffic flow and overall safety for all users.
This project aligns with and supports the broader goals of the US 101/SR 92 Mobility Hub and Smart Corridor Concept Plan. 
Upon completion, it will become one of the City’s first major separated bikeway corridors, providing safe, low-stress travel 
options for people walking, biking, using transit, and driving. The bikeway improvements specifically aim to enhance 
conditions and safety near the freeway on-/off-ramps, incorporate protected intersections, and fill critical gaps in the 
existing bike network. These changes will improve travel times, remove barriers for pedestrians and cyclists, and advance 
multimodal access along this vital corridor linking San Mateo and Foster City.
Funding
The total project cost is estimated at approximately $29 million, with $26 million allocated to construction. The City has 
secured approximately $2.2 million in grant funds for the PA&ED (Project Approval & Environmental Document) and 
design phases, plus $23.1 million for construction. This includes the recently awarded $16.4 million in San Mateo County 
Transportation Authority (SMCTA) Highway Program funds. Based on the current secured funding, a remaining $3 million 
funding gap exists for the construction phase. As part of the SMCTA Highway Program funding recommendation, the 
Transportation Authority also recommended allocating $3 million in Regional Measure 3 (RM3) funds for the project. 
To secure this $3 million in RM3 funds, the City—as the implementation agency—must adopt a Resolution authorizing the 
request to the Metropolitan Transportation Commission (MTC). The language and format of the Resolution is provided by 
SMCTA and MTC.
215 of 424

CITY OF SAN MATEO

A local match is not required for Regional Measure 3 funds.

Subproject Title
19th Ave/Fashion Island Blvd Multimodal Improvements Project
RM3 Funding Amount
$3 million
 
 
I. 
Overall Subproject Information 
a. Project Sponsor / Co-sponsor(s) / Implementing Agency 
• 
San Mateo County Transportation Authority (Sponsor) 
• 
City of San Mateo (Implementing Agency) 
 
b. Detailed Project Description (include definition of deliverable segment if different from overall 
project/subproject) 
The project will provide congestion relief improvements surrounding 19th Ave and Fashion Island Blvd. 
The project will construct a Class IV separated bikeway along the length of the corridor connecting the 
Hayward Park Caltrain Station and future proposed SamTrans Mobility Hub at the existing Caltrans Park 
and Ride lot and optimize signal operations to improve travel flow and increase safety. This project 
supports the broader goals of the 101/92 Mobility Hub and Smart Corridor Concept Plan. Once 
completed, it will be one of the City’s first major separated bikeway corridors, offering safe, low-stress 
travel for all users. 
 
The bikeway improvements are intended to improve conditions and safety near the freeway on-/off-
ramps, develop protected intersections, and fill in gaps in the existing bike network. Additionally, new 
pedestrian walkways will be installed to fill gaps in the sidewalk network, thereby improving travel times 
and removing barriers for people walking along the corridor. These efforts support the multimodal 
220 of 424

--- page 221 ---
Regional Measure 3 Initial Project Report  
Page 2 of 5 
 
improvement of the corridor, relieving congestion in a key corridor connecting San Mateo and Foster 
City within the County. 
 
c. Impediments to Project Completion 
Because the corridor crosses over US-101 and parallels SR-92, the project requires approval from the 
California Department of Transportation (Caltrans) for the portions within State rights-of-way. Caltrans 
must approve the proposed changes to ensure they do not negatively impact the State Highway System 
(SHS). Work at the freeway ramps will require Caltrans’ review and approval of engineering evaluation 
reports and encroachment permits, which can introduce administrative wait times. Existing bridge 
structures limit the width available for new concrete sidewalks and Class IV separated bikeways over 
Seal Slough. Coordinating a design that meets both City of San Mateo multimodal goals and Caltrans’ 
safety standards for highway-adjacent structures is a technical challenge. 
 
d. Risk Management (describe risk management process for project budget and schedule, levels of 
contingency and how they were determined, and risk assessment tools used) 
The most significant risk to the timeline is a delay in the Caltrans approval process or a late-stage design 
rejection from Caltrans. Caltrans may find that the proposed Class IV bike lanes or other operational 
elements create undesirable conditions for the freeway ramps. Mitigation for this risk includes early 
engagement with Caltrans to ensure "no surprises" during the final PS&E (Plans, Specifications, and 
Estimates) phase. The team will also use industry-standard microsimulation modeling to demonstrate 
that multimodal improvements will not result in "significant" Level of Service (LOS) degradation for State 
facilities. Additional time in the schedule has been assumed to accommodate potential revisions. 
Public opposition to the reconfiguration of travel lanes or parking can stall a project indefinitely at the 
City Council level. Vocal residents or business owners may petition to halt the project, citing increased 
commute times or loss of access. To mitigate this risk, the team has developed "before and after" 
visualizations and data to demonstrate the operational and safety benefits from the project. Robust 
outreach and engagement efforts have also been used to ensure transparency with community 
members and to integrate early community feedback into the project.  
 
e. Operability (describe entities responsible for operating and maintaining project once 
completed/implemented) 
The City of San Mateo will be responsible for operating and maintaining the majority of the project area, 
including the traffic signals. The freeway ramps will be operated and maintained by Caltrans, per current 
maintenance agreements between the two agencies. 
 
f. Project Graphic(s) (include below or attach) 
221 of 424

--- page 222 ---
Regional Measure 3 Initial Project Report  
Page 3 of 5 
 
 
 
II. 
Project Phase Description and Status 
a. Environmental/Planning                                                       
       Does NEPA apply? Yes ☒ No☐ 
The project is seeking a categorical exception under NEPA and categorical exemption under CEQA. The 
project qualifies for a Class 1 Exemption pursuant to Section 15301 of the CEQA Guidelines as it meets 
the criteria for a Class 1 Existing Facility project.   
The Preliminary Environmental Study was approved by Caltrans in March 2025. Additional 
documentation has been provided to Caltrans and are under review. NEPA and CEQA approval are 
anticipated by the end of April 2026.  
b. Design 
The 35% design plans were completed in spring 2025. Community feedback was incorporated as part of 
the 65% design plans. Additional community outreach and stakeholder engagement was conducted to 
seek feedback on the design plans. The 100% plans are currently being developed.  
c. Right-of-Way Activities / Acquisition 
Utility coordination is ongoing. No acquisitions or temporary construction easements are anticipated. 
R/W activities include preparing Caltrans exhibits and rights of entries (i.e. no fee letter agreements) to 
allow for conform-related improvements on private property. R/W certification is anticipated by 
Summer 2026. 
d. Construction / Vehicle Acquisition / Operating 
The project is on track for advertising in fall 2026 and start of construction by spring 2027.

necessary. The scope must be consistent with the RM3 statute. If the scope differs from 
the most recent IPR for this project, please describe the reason for any changes here; a 
revised IPR may be necessary. 
The allocation request is for the construction phase. The project will provide congestion relief 
improvements surrounding 19th Ave and Fashion Island Blvd. The project will construct a Class 
IV separated bikeway along the length of the corridor connecting the Hayward Park Caltrain 
Station and future proposed SamTrans Mobility Hub at the existing Caltrans Park and Ride lot 
and optimize signal operations to improve travel flow and increase safety. This project supports 
the broader goals of the 101/92 Mobility Hub and Smart Corridor Concept Plan. Once 
completed, it will be one of the City’s first major separated bikeway corridors, offering safe, low-
stress travel for all users. 
 
The bikeway improvements are intended to improve conditions and safety near the freeway on-
/off-ramps, develop protected intersections, and fill in gaps in the existing bike network. 
Additionally, new pedestrian walkways will be installed to fill gaps in the sidewalk network, 
thereby improving travel times and removing barriers for people walking along the corridor. 
These efforts support the multimodal improvement of the corridor, relieving congestion in a key 
corridor connecting San Mateo and Foster City within the County.

SUBJECT:
Marina Lagoon Spot Dredging Project, Bid Rejection — Approval
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to reject all bids received for the Marina Lagoon Spot Dredging Project.
BACKGROUND:
The Marina Lagoon Spot Dredging Project (Project) is part of the City's plan to perform maintenance on the City's most 
prominent flood control and recreational facility. Since its construction in 1964, the Marina Lagoon has accumulated more 
than 314,190 cubic yards of shoaling (sediment deposition), which has reduced its utility as a flood control and 
recreational facility. Between 1996 and 2024, the flood storage volume in Marina Lagoon has decreased by 19 acre-ft, with 
most of this volume being lost beneath the winter flood management operating levels. Only about 12.5% of sediment 
accumulated over 28 years has impacted active flood storage in the facility. While the City is considering alternatives to 
perform a medium- to large-scale dredging project to address shoaling, this current Project comprises targeted dredging 
up to 2,000 cubic yards from areas where stream and stormwater runoff from where Borel Creek (19th Avenue Channel) 
and Leslie Creek (16th Avenue Channel) flow into Marina Lagoon. This spot dredging effort is expected to improve 
hydraulic connectivity within Marina Lagoon. Dredging near the outfall of Laurel Creek was originally part of the proposed 
project but was later deferred for the medium to large-scale project.
On January 4, 2024, the City issued a Request for Proposals for professional design and professional consultant services for 
the Project. The City awarded the project to Moffatt & Nichol (M&N) on April 1, 2024. M&N subsequently prepared bid 
documents with an Engineer's Estimate of $1,150,000 for the Project. Due to wet weather-work window constraints, staff 
proceeded to advertise the Project prior to receiving final approval by the Dredged Material Management Office (DMMO). 
In accordance with Municipal Code Section 3.60.030, a competitive public process was used to obtain bids for the Project. 
A Notice Inviting Sealed Bids was issued to advertise the Project on June 3, 2025. On the June 25, 2025 bid opening date, 
only one (1) bid was received from Power Engineering Construction Company (Power) with a bid amount of $1,509,500, 
which exceeded the Engineer’s Estimate by $359,500. Pursuant to San Mateo Municipal Code Section 3.60.040(a)(4), the 
City Council, at its discretion, may reject all bids received and authorize the direct negotiation of a contract for the Project, 
if the Project is not within budget, do the work through City employees, or terminate the Project or purchase. 
Given that the bid was greater than the established budget for the Project, staff recommends City Council to reject all bids 
and authorize the Director of Public Works to directly negotiate a potential contract with Power under which cost 
reductions could be achieved. The City did not successfully negotiate a contract with Power.
Therefore, in accordance with Municipal Code Section 3.60.030, a competitive public process was used to obtain bids for 
the Project for a second time. A Notice Inviting Sealed Bids was issued to advertise the Project on January 15, 2026. On the 
February 17, 2026; the bid opening date, two (2) bids were received from Power and Dixon Marine Services, Inc. (Dixon) 
235 of 424

--- page 236 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
with bid amounts of $1,496,000 and $1,438,144, respectively.
Upon review of the received bids, city staff found both bids were missing required information or documentation, and 
therefore both bids were found not responsive.
Staff therefore recommends City Council to reject all bids. Staff plans to re-advertise the project on March 17, 2026, 
pending Council approval to reject bids. Staff still expects the project to be completed in 2026 within the requirements of 
existing regulatory permits.
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no budget impact for the rejection of bids. There are sufficient funds in the Storm System Dredging Project 
(#468005) and the Wet Weather Preparation Activities Project (#46C006) to fund this work provided that the lowest bid of 
$1,438,150 was accepted.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This Project will be performed pursuant to the terms and conditions of an existing permit with the environmental 
regulatory agencies for the Marina Lagoon including the San Francisco Bay Regional Water Quality Control Board. On July 
7, 2021, the City filed a Notice of Exemption with the Office of Planning and Research and the County Clerk-Recorder for 
the County of San Mateo (SCH No. 2021070131) for proposed maintenance activities for the Marina Lagoon, effective from 
2022 through 2026. These maintenance activities are categorically exempt as existing facilities as they involve negligible or 
no expansion of existing or former use. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15301.) 
NOTICE PROVIDED
All meeting noticing requirements were met.

RESOLUTION NO. ____ (2026)
REJECTING ALL BIDS FOR THE MARINA LAGOON SPOT DREDGING PROJECT
WHEREAS, the Marina Lagoon Spot Dredging Project (Project) includes the targeted dredging of up to 
2,000 cubic yards from areas where stream and stormwater runoff from where Borel Creek (19th Avenue 
Channel) and Leslie Creek (16th Avenue Channel) flow into Marina Lagoon; and
WHERAS, work began on the design phase of the Project in April 2024; and
WHEREAS, the City advertised the Project on January 15, 2026 in accordance with Section 3.60.040 of 
the Municipal Code; and
WHEREAS, two bids were received and opened on February 17, 2026 and both bids received were found 
nonresponsive; and
WHEREAS, pursuant to San Mateo Municipal Code section 3.60.040(a)(4), the City Council, at its 
discretion, may reject all bids received.
NOW, THEREFORE, THE CITY COUNCIL OF THE CITY OF SAN MATEO, CALIFORNIA, HEREBY RESOLVES 
that:
1.
This City Council action is exempt from CEQA review, as the targeted dredging of the Marina Lagoon 
of up to 2,000 cubic yards consists of a minor alteration to land where the maintenance dredging of 
land, water, and/or vegetation does not involved removal of healthy, mature, and scenic trees. 
(CEQA Guidelines Section 15304(g).) In addition, the targeted dredging of the Marina Lagoon is 
exempt from CEQA review as an “existing facility,” because it consists of the operation, repair, 
maintenance, permitting, leasing, licensing, or minor alteration of existing public or private 
structures, facilities, mechanical equipment, or topographical features, involving negligible or no 
expansion of existing or former use. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15301.)
2.
In accordance with the provisions of San Mateo Municipal Code Section 3.60.040(a)(4), all bids for 
the Marina Lagoon Spot Dredging Project are rejected.

SUBJECT:
2026 Solid Waste Collection Rates — Rate Increase
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to establish maximum rates for solid waste, recycling, and organics collection for rate year 2026.
BACKGROUND:
The City’s Solid Waste Program (Program) includes waste diversion, compost/recycling education and outreach, 
special collection events, construction and demolition recycling plan processing, litter reduction programs, illegal 
dumping abatement, and hazardous waste management, and ensures compliance with all County, regional, State, 
and Federal regulations. Solid waste, recycling, and organics collection, processing, and disposal services are 
available to all residents and commercial businesses in the City of San Mateo.
 
Each year, Council authorizes the solid waste rates to cover the costs of the collection services provided by Recology 
San Mateo County (Recology); the processing and disposal services provided by South Bayside Industries, Inc. (SBI); 
the City’s portion of the costs of membership in the joint powers authority, South Bayside Waste Management 
Authority (RethinkWaste); and the agency fees, which include the City’s solid waste and street sweeping programs, 
trash capture device maintenance, landfill fee, and franchise fees. The City retains HF&H Consultants (HF&H) to assist 
in developing a rate structure to fund the cost of solid waste services.
 
In 2021, the City adopted a long-term plan for incremental adjustment to the residential solid waste rates to bring 
them into alignment over time with their true cost of service. This is an ongoing effort.
 
For rate year 2026, the City faces increased costs for collection, processing, and disposal services.
The exclusive franchise agreement between the City and Recology San Mateo County allows for inflationary increases 
to Recology’s collection expenses: fuel costs, labor costs, vehicle maintenance costs, and other costs. Recology’s annual 
compensation increases are reviewed and approved by the Board of Directors of RethinkWaste, of which a City Council 
member sits as a director. The agreement was renewed in 2019 and will expire in 2035. Recology’s collection costs in 
2026 increased four percent compared to 2025. After review, the RethinkWaste Board of Directors found the proper 
inflation factors were used to determine Recology’s allowable compensation for 2026 and the increases were 
approved.
 The processing and disposal services fees set by RethinkWaste are increasing seven percent in 2026. The cost increase is a
result of a five-percent increase to the tipping fee rates set by RethinkWaste and increased amounts of material from San 
Mateo being processed at the Shoreway Facility.
 
238 of 424

--- page 239 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
For 2026, in addition to collection, processing, and disposal costs and City program fees, solid waste rates include funding 
$150,000 annually to be set aside in the Solid Waste Fund dedicated to the future purchase of replacement street 
sweepers. 
 
The 2026 cost increase is also a result of the decision of the City Council last year to defer $331,333 in increased costs 
from 2025 to 2026, as allowed under the franchise agreement. That decision lowered rates in 2025 but must be 
accounted for this year.
 
Maintaining current rates to meet the 2026 projected expenses described above would lead to an anticipated deficit of
$1,170,910.  Therefore, staff recommends the City Council to authorize raising residential rates by a range of $4.16-$6.50 
per month. These increases will result in a projected deficit of $14,137 in 2026. In addition, residential customers 
subscribing to 96-gallon solid waste carts will meet their cost of service.
 
Table A summarizes the current 2025 and proposed 2026 residential rates. These rates include collection, processing, and 
disposal of garbage, recycling, and organics.
 
Table A – Proposed 2026 Residential Solid Waste Rate Changes

Staff Recommendation:
Based on the above, staff recommends Council authorize the rate increases in Table A to meet the need for commercial 
and residential rates to meet their cost of service, to judiciously preserve the Solid Waste Fund reserves to blunt the 
impact of future rate increases as well as the City’s anticipated share of future capital improvements at the Shoreway 
Environmental Center forecast by RethinkWaste, and to be better prepared for upcoming, anticipated increases from 
Recology, South Bayside Industries, and RethinkWaste.
 
Balancing Account:
In accordance with the City’s franchise agreement, Recology maintains a balancing account which tracks the cumulative 
surplus or deficit between what Recology is owed and what is collected from solid waste rates. The City is charged interest 
if the cumulative balancing account is a negative balance at the end of any given year. Past City Council direction is to 
avoid interest charges by keeping the balancing account as low as possible.
 
In March 2024, the City Council authorized the use of $1.0 million in fund reserves to be remitted to Recology to reduce 
the balancing account deficit before the year’s end. The payment was made in August 2024 which avoided accruing 
additional interest on the deficit. In March 2025, the City Council authorized the use of $0.4 million to be remitted to 
Recology to reduce the balancing account deficit. The payment was made in August 2025 which avoided accruing 
additional interest on the deficit.
 
The City’s current long-term plan is to bring residential rates into alignment with their cost of service by the end of the
current franchise agreement in 2035. By judiciously using Solid Waste Fund reserves over time to blunt the effect on 
residential rate payers, this goal is attainable. These funds are derived from rate stabilization fees collected from 2019-
2021 and from City program savings over time.
 
Council direction has been for all solid waste and street sweeping program savings to be used for rate stabilization. In 
March 2025, staff reported to Council that $4.4 million was available for stabilization.
239 of 424

--- page 240 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
 
Since then, the City has allocated approximately $630,000 to purchase a new electric street sweeper, the purchase for 
which was approved by City Council on November 18, 2024, and is considering purchase of a bike lane electric street 
sweeper which would be of a similar cost. These purchases would reduce the reserve balance to $3.2 million for future 
rate stabilization. However, with the recommended increases to residential rates, no remittance to Recology is 
recommended for 2026. City staff will continue to recommend strategic use of Solid Waste Fund reserves to help offset 
added costs for service and/or partially offset future rate increases as the cost-of-service implementation plan is 
implemented.
It is important to note that in addition to potential annual contractual inflationary increases, Recology, SBI, and 
RethinkWaste must meet state regulations such as the move to zero emission vehicles. RethinkWaste is considering 
necessary capital improvement projects and bond measures, all of which will likely require the City to raise rates in the 
next few years.
Rate Setting Procedures:
Proposition 218 is a citizen-led initiative constitutional amendment enacted in 1996 and is alternatively known as the 
“Right to Vote on Taxes Act.” While the courts have not ruled that Proposition 218 applies to solid waste collection 
rates from a franchise hauler, in an abundance of caution, the City has historically followed the “majority protest” 
proceedings set forth in Proposition 218.
 
Accordingly, with the City Council’s approval, a public notification of the proposed maximum increase in solid waste 
rates was mailed to property owners on January 16, 2026 (Attachment 2). The notification instructed property owners 
on how to submit a valid written protest. Valid written protests are accepted through the time of this public hearing. 
 
At the end of the public hearing, the City Clerk will tally and report the qualifying written protests and the City Council 
will certify whether the written protests in opposition to the increase meet or do not meet the majority protest 
threshold. In accordance with Article XIIID, Section 6, of the California Constitution, a "majority protest" exists if written 
protests against the proposed fee or charge are presented by a majority of owners of the identified parcels. If a 
majority protest is not received, the City Council may then authorize the increased rates. If a majority protest is 
received, the City cannot increase the rates and the rates will remain unchanged.
Assuming a majority protest does not exist, staff recommends Council adopt the Proposed Resolution (Attachment 1) 
establishing the above-referenced solid waste rates.
BUDGET IMPACT:
If the proposed rate adjustments are authorized, the Solid Waste Fund’s revenues will increase toward alignment with 
the costs of service.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This action by the City in setting the maximum rates to be charged by Recology San Mateo County relates to funding an on-
going solid waste collection service and is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is a government fiscal activity which 
does not involve any commitment to any specific project which may result in a potentially significant physical impact on 
the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(4).)
NOTICE PROVIDED
Notice of the proposed maximum increase in solid waste rates and the March 16, 2026, notice of public hearing were 
mailed to all property owners and Recology customers on January 16, 2026. This meets the 45-day notification 
requirement.

RESOLUTION NO. __ (2026)
ADOPTING INCREASED MAXIMUM RESIDENTIAL AND COMMERCIAL SOLID WASTE RATES FOR 2026
WHEREAS, the City reviews the rates charged by the City’s franchisee (hereafter “Solid Waste Rates”); and
WHEREAS, the purpose of the rate review is to ensure that rates cover the cost of solid waste, recycling, 
compost collection and disposal, and other fees; and
WHEREAS, the South Bayside Waste Management Association (SBWMA) approved Recology’s 
Compensation Application for rate year 2026 including a rate increase, incorporated herein by reference; 
and
WHEREAS, a rate increase for 2026 is proposed in an amount not-to-exceed $6.00 a month for all 
residential accounts with 20-gallon cart service, not-to-exceed $6.25 a month for all residential accounts with 
32-gallon cart service, not-to-exceed $6.50 a month for all residential accounts with 64-gallon cart service, and 
not-to-exceed $4.16 per month for all residential accounts with 96-gallon cart service; and
WHEREAS, while not currently required by law, the City followed the noticing procedure set forth in 
Article XIII D, Section 6 of the California Constitution (Proposition 218); and
WHEREAS, in accordance with Proposition 218, the City Council authorized staff to proceed with 
noticing and protest proceedings for the proposed rate increase; and
WHEREAS, the public was noticed of such proposed Solid Waste Rate increases by a notice mailed to all 
property owners and rate payers within the City on January 16, 2026; and
WHEREAS, a public hearing was held on March 16, 2026; and 
WHEREAS, it was determined that there was not a majority protest.
NOW, THEREFORE, THE CITY COUNCIL OF THE CITY OF SAN MATEO, CALIFORNIA, HEREBY FINDS and
RESOLVES that:
1.
Pursuant to California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) Guidelines Section 15378(b)(4), this action is 
not a project under CEQA because the City’s rate setting relates to the funding mechanism of on-going 
solid waste collection services, which does not involve any commitment to any specific project which 
may result in a potentially significant physical impact on the environment.
2.
In accordance with Article XIII D, Section 6 of the California Constitution:
a.
The revenues derived from the Solid Waste Rates will not exceed the funds required to provide 
solid waste services.
b.
The revenues derived from the Solid Waste Rates will not be used for any purpose other than solid 
waste services.
c.
The Solid Waste Rate charged will not exceed the cost of solid waste services per parcel.
242 of 424

--- page 243 ---
d.
Any surplus monies collected from the solid waste fees will be used to offset future revenue 
requirements.
e.
The Solid Waste Rate is only charged for solid waste services that are currently available.
f.
The Solid Waste Rate is not charged for general governmental services.
3.
Notice of the proposed increase in maximum solid waste collection rates to be charged by the 
franchisee was mailed to property owners on January 16, 2026.
4.
A public hearing was conducted on March 16, 2026, regarding the proposed maximum Solid Waste 
Rates to be charged by the franchisee and all protests and other comments were considered.
5.
The number of written protests by owners of parcels within the City against the increase in maximum 
rates to be charged by the franchisee was tabulated by the City Clerk and the number of protests fell 
short of a majority.
6.

the residential and commercial solid waste program, set forth in the attached Exhibit A.
7.
The new maximum $6.00 per month increase for Solid Waste Rates to be charged by the City’s franchisee 
for all residential accounts with 20-gallon cart service shall be effective April 1, 2026.
8.

franchisee for all residential accounts with 32-gallon cart service shall be effective April 1, 2026.
9.
The new maximum $6.50 per month increase for Solid Waste Rates to be charged by the City’s 
franchisee for all residential accounts with 64-gallon cart service shall be effective April 1, 2026.

for the residential and commercial solid waste program if required.
12. The new maximum Solid Waste Rates to be charged by the City’s franchisee for residential users 
shall be effective April 1, 2026.

PROPOSED INCREASE FOR SOLID WASTE/RECYCLING/COMPOST COLLECTION FOR RATE YEAR 2026 
NOTICE OF PUBLIC HEARING 
March 16, 2026, 7:00 pm City of San Mateo City Council Chambers 330 West 20th Avenue, San Mateo, CA 94403 
HOW SOLID WASTE RATES ARE DETERMINED 
Solid waste rates include the costs for collection services provided by Recology San 
Mateo County (Recology), processing and disposal services provided by the South 
Bayside Waste Management Authority (SBWMA), and City fees that support the City’s 
sustainability goals and efforts to meet all regulatory compliance mandates. These 
include the solid waste program, street sweeping, litter and illegal dumping 
abatement, trash capture device management, and franchise fees. Customer rates are 
adjusted as required to generate enough revenue to cover the cost of the provided 
services. These services are available to or provided as a benefit to all City of San 
Mateo residents and commercial businesses. Recommended rates are reviewed by the 
RethinkWaste Board of Directors based on a financial evaluation of operating costs 
and projected revenues. Single family residential bills are based on the size of the 
solid waste cart and cover the cost of collection, processing, and disposal of solid 
waste, recycling, and organic material. Commercial and multi-family rates are based 
on the size of the solid waste container and frequency of collection (e.g., 1 time per 
week, 2 times per week) and covers the cost of collecting solid waste and recycling. 
In addition, commercial and multi-family rates include a charge for organic material 
collections based on the size and frequency of collection of the organic material 
container. 
The full rate schedule, including all commercial services, is available for review at 
www.cityofsanmateo.org/ProposedSolidWasteRates or at the City Clerk’s Office at 
330 West 20th Avenue, San Mateo. 
PROPOSED RATE INCREASES 
The proposed change is directly related to the increases to collection, disposal, and 
processing costs which have caused costs to exceed revenues. 
IMPACT OF PROPOSED INCREASE ON SOLID WASTE BILLS 
The following table summarizes the rates with proposed increases. 
All commercial/multi-family garbage rates will remain flat.  
Recology provides additional services upon request that are not included in the 
City’s solid waste collection rate. Additional services include backyard or distance 
service, additional carts, and extra on-call pickups of bulk items. The fees for 
optional services will appear on the customer’s Recology San Mateo County bill 
as separate line items in addition to the standard cost of collection. A complete 
list of all the additional services and their 2026 cost is available at 
www.cityofsanmateo.org/ProposedSolidWasteRates or at the City Clerk’s Office 
located at 330 W. 20th Avenue, San Mateo.

The City of San Mateo is currently considering an increase to 
the charges set by the City and collected by Recology for solid 
waste/recycling/compost collection and disposal. You are 
receiving this notice because City records indicate that you 
are a solid waste customer receiving service in the City. 
On March 16, 2026 at 7:00 p.m., or as soon thereafter as the 
matter may be heard, the San Mateo City Council will hold a 
Public Hearing to accept public input and testimony on a 
proposed increase on the maximum allowable charge for solid 
waste/recycling/compost collection and disposal, to be effective 
April 1, 2026. Any interested person may present verbal or 
written input to the City Council. Although the City Council will 
consider all input, State law provides that only the written 
protests of property owners may be counted to determine 
whether a majority protest (50% of all parcels +1) to the 
proposed maximum exists. If, at the conclusion of the public 
hearing, a majority protest of property owners does not exist, 
the City Council will consider adoption of the proposed increase 
in the maximum allowable charges.

FOR IMPORTANT DETAILS

HOW TO PROTEST THE RATE INCREASE 
A property owner or a City solid waste customer may submit a 
written protest to the proposed increase in maximum rates to be 
charged by the City’s franchisee by U.S. mail, to be received prior 
to the meeting date, or hand delivered to the City Clerk, at any 
time before the end of the public hearing. The City Clerk’s 
address is: 330 West 20th Avenue, San Mateo, CA 94403. Only 
one protest per parcel will be counted. Written protest must 
include the owner or customer’s name, service address, 
assessor’s parcel number (the nine-digit number that appears on 
your property tax statement) and the owner’s or customer’s 
signature for the property served. While protests may not be 
submitted by electronic means such as email or texts, as they 
are not an official rate protest mechanism under Proposition 218, 
the City values all residential input and will read all 
correspondence regarding rates.

San Mateo City Council February 15, 2026
330 West 20th Avenue
San Mateo, CA 94403

RE: Opposing Recology’s Additional $6 Monthly Increase for the 20-Gallon Cart
Dear Councilmembers,

| am writing to express my strong opposition to the proposed additional $6.00 per month
increase for the 20-gallon residential garbage cart. | urge the Council to reject this portion of
the rate proposal or, at a minimum, require Recology to restructure the increase so that it aligns
with cost-of-service principles and the City’s environmental goals.

Below are the key reasons this increase is neither fair nor consistent with the City’s stated
policies.

1. The 20-galion cart exists to reward waste reduction — raising its price undermines that
purpose

The smallest cart is designed to incentivize residents to generate less landfill waste and comply
with state mandates such as SB 1383. Increasing the cost of the 20-gallon cart at a higher
proportional rate than larger carts punishes the very households who are doing exactly what
the City has asked them to do: reduce waste, compost properly, and minimize landfill tonnage.

A rate structure that discourages downsizing contradicts both local and statewide
environmental policy.

2. The 20-gallon cart already absorbed a steep increase in 2025

In 2025, the 20-gallon cart saw a $7.50/month increase, one of the largest single-year jumps in
recent memory. Adding another $6.00 now would mean a cumulative increase of
$13.50/month in just two years — a disproportionate burden on the smallest service tier.

No other essential utility in the City has seen increases of this magnitude for its lowest-usage
customers.

3. The smallest cart has the lowest cost to serve

The 20-gallon cart:


--- page 252 ---
® uses the least truck capacity

® generates the least landfill tonnage

e incurs the lowest disposal and processing costs

e contributes minimally to contamination issues
Cost-of-service principles require that rate increases be tied to the actual cost of providing
service. A nearly flat increase across all cart sizes is not cost-reflective and results in the
smallest, lowest-impact customers subsidizing higher-volume users.

4. A $6 increase is disproportionately large in percentage terms

For a 20-gallon cart currently around $36/month, a $6 increase represents roughly a 17% hike.
For a 64-gallon cart around $72/month, the same $6 is only an 8.5% increase.

This structure is regressive and places the heaviest burden on the smallest households.
5. Many 20-gallon customers are seniors, single-person households, and low-waste families
These residents often:

e live on fixed incomes
e generate minimal waste
e have already invested time and effort in proper sorting and composting

The City has historically used the 20-gallon tier to protect these groups. This increase reverses
that longstanding policy and disproportionately impacts those least able to absorb repeated
rate hikes.

6. Recology’s cited cost drivers do not scale equally across cart sizes
If Recology attributes the increase to:

© fuel
e labor
® organics processing
e contamination
e SB 1383 compliance
..those costs are not driven by 20-gallon customers. They are driven by volume, contamination,

and tonnage — all significantly higher in larger carts.

A uniform or near-uniform increase across all cart sizes is not justified by the underlying cost
structure,

7. The City has a responsibility to ensure ratepayer fairness, not simply pass through requests


--- page 253 ---
The Council is not obligated to approve every proposed increase. Rate-setting requires
evaluating:

@ proportionality
e equity
e cost-of-service alignment
¢ consistency with environmental goals
A steep increase for the smallest cart fails multiple criteria and does not meet the City’s

obligation to protect residents from disproportionate impacts.
8. The increase exceeds inflation and typical utility cost trends

With inflation in the 3-4% range, a 17% increase for the smallest cart is far out of alignment
with economic conditions. Essential services should not outpace inflation by a factor of four or
five, especially for the lowest-usage tier.

9. Recology already receives revenue from every CRV-eligible bottle and can collected in our
blue bins.

They redeem that CRV through the state system and also earn money from selling the
aluminum, plastic, and glass. Those revenues are specifically designed to offset recycling costs.
Until Recology fully accounts for how this income is used, there is no justification for an
additional rate increase — especially on the smallest, lowest-waste customers.

Conclusion

For all these reasons, | respectfully request that the Council reject the proposed $6 increase for
the 20-gallon cart or require Recology to restructure the rate proposal so that increases are
proportional to actual cost drivers and consistent with the City’s environmental and equity
goals.

Thank you for your attention to this matter and for your continued service to our community.

CITY OF SAN MATEO
Agenda Report
City Hall
330 W. 20th Avenue
San Mateo CA 94403
www.cityofsanmateo.org
Agenda Number:  23
Section Name: NEW BUSINESS
Account Number: 25-3112
File ID: 26-10907
TO:
City Council
FROM:
Alex Khojikian, City Manager
PREPARED BY:
Community Development Department
MEETING DATE:
March 16, 2026 
SUBJECT:
2025 Annual Progress Reports – Housing Element, General Plan and Housing Successor Agency – Approval
RECOMMENDATION:
Approve the Annual Progress Reports and direct the City Manager to submit the 2025 General Plan Annual Progress 
Report to the Governor's Office of Land Use and Climate Innovation; the 2025 Housing Element Annual Progress Report to 
the California Department of Housing and Community Development; and the 2025 Housing Successor Agency Annual 
Report to the California Department of Finance. 
BACKGROUND:
Government Code Sections 65400 and 65700 require local planning agencies to submit an Annual Progress Report (APR) 
on the status of their General Plan and Housing Element implementation to the local legislative body. The APR is then 
submitted to California Department of Housing and Community Development (HCD) and Governor’s Office of Land Use 
and Climate Innovation (LUCI; formerly the Office of Policy and Research (OPR)) by April 1st of each year. 
California Health and Safety Code Section 34176.1(f) similarly provides for jurisdictions to submit an annual Housing 
Successor Agency Report by the same deadline to the California Department of Finance (DoF). This report provides details 
of the housing funds, assets, and activities of the City of San Mateo, which is acting as the Housing Successor Agency of the 
former Redevelopment Agency of the City of San Mateo.
This agenda report contains the General Plan and Housing Element Annual Progress Reports (Attachments 1 and 2) and 
Housing Successor Annual Report (Attachment 3) for review and approval by City Council. An overview of each of the three 
state-mandated reports is provided below.
Housing Element Annual Progress Report Summary
While the Housing Element is a part of the General Plan, the Housing Element APR is subject to additional and specific
requirements not applicable to other elements of the General Plan. The Housing Element APR has been prepared pursuant 
to California Government Code Section 65400 and includes a detailed accounting of all housing projects submitted, 
reviewed, entitled, and constructed in the City in calendar year 2025. The APR also provides an update on the 
implementation of each of the Housing Element’s policies and programs to achieve the City’s housing vision and remain in 
compliance with State law. 
 
The City’s 2023-2031 Housing Element for the sixth housing cycle was originally adopted by the City Council on May 20, 
2024 and determined to be in substantial compliance with Housing Element law by HCD on July 23, 2024. On February 23, 
2026, the City Council adopted Housing Element Amendments that updated the sites inventory to incorporate new 
pipeline projects and remove projects that were no longer active, made changes related to the Measure T voter approval 
and the settlement of a lawsuit, revised the realistic capacity of opportunity sites based on new information, and made 
276 of 424

--- page 277 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
minor changes to some of the implementation plan action items. HCD found these amendments to be in substantial 
conformance with State law on December 18, 2025. The City is now awaiting certification from HCD, following the Council 
adoption in February. 
This APR (Attachment 1) is for the third year of the City’s 2023-2031 Housing Element, and it includes the following 
information that the City has data to report on:

Information on number of planning applications submitted, as well as those that were approved (Table A) 

Information on the annual building activity of new construction including units entitled, permitted, and completed 
with the types of housing units and affordability level (Table A2) 

Progress in meeting the City’s Regional Housing Needs Allocation (RHNA) by income level (Table B)

Progress report on implementation of the Housing Element programs (Attachment 1 - Table D)

Information on locally-owned surplus sites (Table H)

Information on the City’s local preference policy (Table K)

Historical resources (Table L)
Progress Towards RHNA Targets
The City continued to make meaningful progress towards its RHNA in 2025, including the following achievements:
•
477 9th Ave., a mixed-use project on a former office site that includes 120 rental housing units, with 12 very low-
income BMR units, received its building permit early in 2025 and is currently under construction. 
•
Peninsula Heights, a 290-unit multi-family for-sale housing development on a former office campus site that 
includes 29 low-income BMR units, to be built in phases, continued to make progress in 2025 by receiving building 
permits for seven single-family homes (market rate).
•
Bay Meadows Mixed-Use Block MU3, which includes a multi-family building with 67 rental units, with seven low-
income BMR units, received its building permit in 2025, but construction has not yet started.
•
124 Accessory Dwelling Unit (ADU) applications were submitted and 94 ADU permits issued.

Accessory Dwelling Units
Following adoption of the City’s ADU Ordinance on March 21, 2022, the City Council directed staff to provide annual 
updates on the implementation of the Ordinance. Any ADU that meets the standards outlined in the City’s adopted ADU 
Ordinance is approved ministerially (building permit only). However, to allow for flexibility and creative ADU designs, the 
City offers a discretionary permit application option through an Accessory Dwelling Unit Discretionary Review (ADUDR) 
Planning Application process to consider proposals for ADUs that are not in full compliance with the development 
standards in the ADU Ordinance. It should be noted that the vast majority of ADU proposals conform to these 
development standards and are processed ministerially (over 300 permits issued), with only 11 being approved utilizing 
the ADUDR.
The City began processing ADUDR applications after adoption of the ADU Ordinance in 2022, and a total of 19 ADUDR 
applications have been submitted since then. Of the 19 total applications, six proposed the expansion of an existing 
structure within a legal non-conforming setback of less than four feet from a property line, seven proposed a second-story 
balcony, and four proposed a height exceedance. Eleven of the 19 submittals have been approved, one is currently under 
review, and seven were withdrawn. For each of the withdrawn ADUDR applications, the applicant revised, or 
communicated intentions to revise, the proposal to conform to ADU development standards and resubmit for a ministerial 
permit. Currently, per Council direction, ADUDR applications are processed at no additional cost to the applicant. This no 
cost approach is considered a pilot program, and staff will bring the topic back to Council for review later, likely as part of 
the fee study that will be initiated later this year.
278 of 424

--- page 279 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
The City committed to amending the ADU Ordinance to align it with current State law and to resolve any inconsistencies 
identified by HCD as part of the implementation plan (Policy H 1.4). HCD has statutory authority to review local agencies’ 
ADU Ordinances, and as of January 1, 2024, also has authority to enforce all State ADU laws. In October of 2024, the City 
received a letter from HCD with a review of the City’s ADU Ordinance, identifying areas that required updates or 
clarification for full compliance with State ADU Law. Staff met with HCD and provided an official response to HCD’s letter in 
November 2024, with a target to update the ADU Ordinance by the end of 2025.  On August 26, 2025, a Planning 
Commission study session was held to introduce ADU Ordinance updates and receive community and Commission 
feedback. Following the study session, staff conducted outreach and hosted a virtual workshop on local ADU needs and 
requirements on September 25, 2025. On December 9, 2025, the Planning Commission held a public hearing to review the 
draft ADU Ordinance amendments and recommended City Council adoption. On January 26, 2025, the City Council 
introduced the ADU Ordinance and it was adopted on February 2, 2026. Pursuant to State law, staff submitted the 
Ordinance to HCD for official review following adoption. As of the date of this report, there have not been any comments 
from HCD.
In addition to reporting on the number of ADU applications, jurisdictions must also include data in the APR on the 
affordability of ADUs. In previous years, HCD allowed cities to distribute ADUs into income categories using assumptions 
based on a statewide survey but had indicated disallowing further use of the data because it was outdated.  Beginning in 
2025, San Mateo required applicants to complete an ADU rental survey prior to permit issuance for ADUs. The survey was 
created in a regional partnership with the Association of Bay Area Governments (ABAG) as a tool for participating 
jurisdictions to track ADU affordability. The data collected from this survey was aggregated by ABAG such that homeowner 
responses are anonymous. ABAG submitted the results of the rental survey to HCD for review, and HCD determined the 
previous affordability assumptions for ABAG jurisdictions (30 percent above moderate, 30 percent moderate, 30 percent 
low, and 10 percent very low) could continue to be used since the results of the recent rental survey were similar to 
previous surveys.
The City has continued to make meaningful progress in ADU production, with 94 ADU permits issued in 2025. This is well 
above the annual estimate of 55 building permits for ADUs per the 2023-2031 Housing Element. According to HCD’s 
statewide APR Dashboard, the City leads the county in terms of ADU permitting. Table 3 highlights the number of ADUs 
permitted between 2023 and 2025. Since 2023, the City has issued permits for 243 ADUs, which is 55% of the total ADUs 
assigned by RHNA in the eight-year period for the Sixth Housing Cycle.

SB 9 Projects
Following adoption of the City’s SB 9 Ordinance on October 3, 2022, the City Council directed staff to provide annual 
updates on the implementation of the Ordinance. SB 9 generally allows the building of up to two primary dwellings on a 
single-family lot and ADUs and JADU allowed under state law and/or the subdivision of a single-family lot into two lots. 
Any development that meets the standards outlined in the City’s adopted SB 9 Ordinance is approved ministerially. 
However, to allow for flexibility and creative designs, the City offers a discretionary permit application option through a 
Single-Family Dwelling Design Review (SFDDR) Planning Application process to consider proposals for dwellings that are 
not in full compliance with the SB 9 development standards. 
279 of 424

--- page 280 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
The City began processing SB 9 applications after adoption of the Ordinance in 2022. Since that time, a total of seven SB 9 
applications have been submitted. Of the seven applications, three were approved ministerially and four required 
discretionary review. These discretionary reviews were required to evaluate SB 9 units over 800 square feet in size, which 
is the maximum unit size that can be proposed under a ministerial SB 9 application. Of these four discretionary reviews, 
one was approved by the Zoning Administrator and subsequently appealed to the Planning Commission, which upheld the 
Zoning Administrator approval. The other three discretionary SB 9 applications are currently incomplete, requiring 
additional review before final action can be taken. An overview of SB9 Discretionary Planning Applications received to-date 
is provided below in Table 4. All ministerially reviewed applications were approved by the Zoning Administrator.

Goals, Policies, and Programs
The City has also made progress on implementation of the goals, policies, and programs outlined in the Housing Element. 
The following is a list of the significant policies and programs that began or progressed in 2025: 
•
Tenant Protections/Anti-Displacement (Policies H3.4, H3.6 and H5.4.1)
Following community engagement and research on tenant protections and anti-displacement in San Mateo 
conducted throughout 2024, the City Council held a study session on April 21, 2025. 
o
To implement Policy H3.4, City Council directed staff to develop a policy requiring property owners to 
provide relocation assistance to tenants with a rental tenure of 11 months or longer in no-fault evictions 
and, for substantial rehabilitation evictions, provide notification when the remodeled unit becomes 
available. City Council also directed staff to amend the City’s Tenant Relocation Ordinance (San Mateo 
Municipal Code 7.50) to strengthen the City’s ability to support tenants displaced from unsafe or 
substandard units. The ordinances implementing Policy H3.4 were adopted in December 2025.
o
While not included as a Housing Element policy, City Council directed staff to develop an emergency rental 
assistance program in response to the finding that the majority of eviction cases in San Mateo are related 
to nonpayment of rent. The Emergency Rental Assistance Program, which is administered by Samaritan 
House, was created in July 2025 with an initial fund of $168,000. As part of the recently adopted Housing 
Element amendments, a policy to recognize this program was added to the Housing Element.
o
To implement policy H3.6, City directed staff to build on existing City data collection through its Business 
License Tax to monitor rental rates and evictions citywide, rather than through adoption of a formal rental 
registry. Staff continues to work on rental data collection through its business tax platform. 
•
Zoning Code Amendment Ordinance – Phase I (several Policies)  
There were several amendments to the Zoning Code for policy programs that had prescribed actions in the 
implementation plan targeted for adoption in 2024 and 2025. For efficiency purposes, these proposed code 
amendments were aggregated into one ordinance for adoption (Phase I), with a second bundled amendments 
adopted later in 2025 (Phase II). On April 7, 2025, the Council adopted an ordinance that included the following:
o
Allowed residential uses as permitted uses in commercial districts and office districts. (Policy H1.7)
o
Added a definition for single room occupancy (SRO) and amend the Zoning Code to allow SROs in the R4 
280 of 424

--- page 281 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
District (will also apply to R5, R6 and Downtown Districts with an R overlay). (Policy H1.7)
o
Eliminated the need for special use permits (SUPs) for multiple-family housing in mixed-use zoning 
districts. (Policy H1.7)
o
Removed Chapter 27.17 (Manufactured Homes) and simplify development standards for manufactured 
homes on a permanent foundation to be the same as single-family homes. (Policy H1.7)
o
Removed any prohibitions of accessory dwelling units (ADUs) in zoning districts as a permitted use per 
State law. (Policy H1.7) 
o
Added new Zoning Code terms and definitions related to housing and update existing definitions to add 
clarity and be consistent with State law. (Policies H1.7, H1.13, H1.14, H1.15, H1.16, and H3.7) 
o
Amended the Zoning Code to allow group homes and residential care facilities for people with special 
needs in all residential zones and only subject to those objective standards that apply to other residential 
uses of the same type in the same zone in conformance with state law. (Policy 1.13)
o
Amended the Zoning Code to define and allow farmworker housing within the Agricultural District 
consistent with state law. (Policy H1.16)
o
Modified the “emergency shelter” definition and added development standards to the definition; added 
new terms and definitions for supportive housing and low-barrier navigation centers; allowed for 
emergency shelters, supportive housing, and low-barrier navigation centers in certain zoning districts per 
State law. (Policy 3.7)
o
Modified the Powers and Duties of the Zoning Administrator to review and take final action on proposed 
residential development projects that are consistent with the ODS by increasing the project size threshold 
from 25 units to 99 units. (Policy H1.6) 
Additional Zoning Code amendments will be forthcoming as part of the Comprehensive Zoning Code Update, 
which is currently underway, or as separate action items as targeted in the implementation plan.
•
General Plan Implementation (Policy H 1.20)  
On April 7, 2025, the Council adopted an ordinance to amend the General Provisions section of the Zoning Code 
relating to implementation of Measure T and General Plan consistency. The provisions incorporate the uses, 
densities, intensities, and heights specified in Land Use Element of General Plan 2040 (Measure T) where they may 
conflict with the existing Zoning Code and establishes the standards in General Plan 2040 as governing until the 
Comprehensive Zoning Code Update has been completed.
•
Zoning Code Amendment Ordinance – Phase II (Policies H1.6 and H1.9)  
For this bundle of Zoning Code amendments, several chapters of the Zoning Code were amended to implement 
programs to support the goal of housing production within the Housing Element. On July 28, 2025, the City Council 
adopted an ordinance that amended and added several sections of the Zoning Code to reduce the development 
review timeline for housing projects by streamlining the development review process, aligning City requirements 
with SB 330, and adding certainty in the development application process. The ordinance established an appeals 
process when a planning application is determined to be ‘incomplete’ subject to the review and determination of 
the Planning Commission. The ordinance amended a code section to allow the Zoning Administrator to approve up 
to two extensions to a planning application approval, each for up to two years, in addition to the regular 2.5-year 
expiration from the approval date. This allows developers to complete the construction of the project as approved, 
which is also in the City’s interest to satisfy the housing production targets in the Housing Element. An additional 
measure to streamline the development review process was to change the requirement for City Council approval 
authority of proposed residential projects only when they exceed the building height limit specified in General Plan 
2040 (all other projects are subject to Zoning Administrator or Planning Commission approval per Phase I 
amendments). Finally, the ordinance established minimum densities for all residential mixed-use development 
projects that are proposed in the mixed-use land use designations established in General Plan 2040, with slightly 
higher minimum densities for areas within ½-mile of a Caltrain station.
281 of 424

--- page 282 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
•
Alternative Affordable Housing Compliance for Housing Development Projects (Policy H1.3)
Policy H.1.3 is one of many actions in the Housing Element intended to increase affordable housing production by 
providing greater flexibility to developers. The City’s Below Market Rate (BMR) Guidelines require 15 percent of 
total units (base density) in a housing development of 11 or more units to be provided as affordable to lower-
income or moderate-income households, depending on whether the development is rental or for-sale. While the 
previous BMR Guidelines allowed for an offsite alternative compliance option, it was limited to cases in 
which building the required affordable units on-site was infeasible, as demonstrated by the applicant with an 
economic analysis. The BMR Ordinance (San Mateo Municipal Code 27.16.050) and Guidelines were updated on 
June 16, 2025, to allow for offsite units or a developer-proposed alternative.
General Plan Annual Progress Report Summary
The General Plan is a State-mandated document that sets the framework for the City’s physical, social, and economic
development into the future. The City’s General Plan APR (Attachment 2) highlights the accomplishments in 2025 in 
implementing land use related goals, policies, and actions. Through the accomplishments highlighted in the APR, the City 
continues to make progress to achieve a healthy economic, social, and physical environment to serve the San Mateo 
community. The APR includes the following key information:

Planning activities initiated or completed by the City in 2025;

Highlights of major planning applications approved in 2025;

Building permit and construction status of major development projects; and

Implementation summary of General Plan goals, policies and actions pertaining to land use.
Consistent with General Plan Action LU 15.3, the City is providing population information from the California Department 
of Finance (DoF). These population estimates are produced annually by DoF to assist local areas in calculating their annual 
appropriations limit. Changes to the housing stock are used in the preparation of the annual city population estimates. 
Additionally, population levels are determined by estimated housing unit occupancy and the average number of persons 
per household. These estimates are based on data through January 1, 2025. The City's population has increased by 0.1 
percent, from 104,221 on January 1, 2024, to 104,315 on January 1, 20253.
Pursuant to General Plan Policies H 1.20, LU 1.2 and LU 1.3 – In November 2024, following the adoption of General Plan 
2040, Measure T was approved by the voters to fully allow the heights, densities and intensities permitted in the Land Use 
Element to be implemented. Measure T allows the City to meet the community’s anticipated housing needs through 2040 
as well as housing allocations (RHNAs) the City will be required to plan for by HCD for both current and future housing 
cycles between now and 2040. The City is working on a Comprehensive Zoning Code Update to implement General Plan 
2040 and Measure T.
Pursuant to Action LU 1.10, the City internally tracks nonresidential development and housing units by affordability levels 
for all major planning applications. This data will be used in the coming years to determine if City plans need to be 
adjusted or if the city-wide job-housing balance needs to be re-evaluated. Staff is currently working on developing a 
dashboard to provide public access to this data and anticipates reporting on this action as part of a future year General 
Plan APR. 
Housing Successor Report Summary
The purpose of the Housing Successor Report (Attachment 3) is to provide the governing body of the Housing Successor an 
annual report on the housing assets and activities of the City of San Mateo acting as the Housing Successor of the former 
Redevelopment Agency of the City of San Mateo as required by State law for the fiscal year July 1, 2024 to June 30, 2025.
 
At the end of the FY 24/25 reporting period, the fund balance was $5,448,369. Per State law Section 34176.1(a)(2)(A) of 
the California Health and Safety Code, up to $500,000 in Low/Moderate Housing Income Asset Funds may be used to

prevent homelessness. In FY 25/26, the City began using Low/Moderate Housing Income Asset funds to support the new 
Emergency Rental Assistance Program ($168,000) administered by Samaritan House and the City’s Homeless Outreach 
Team ($190,000), operated out of the Police Department. The HOT was previously funded entirely out of the General 
Fund.

H1.1 Monitor Regional 
Housing Needs Allocation 
and Pipeline projects
a)   Provide an annual report on housing production to the City Council each March, 
prior to submittal of the Annual Progress Report to HCD by April 1st. The report will 
include an assessment on the City’s RHNA progress and, as needed, identify additional 
sites to ensure ongoing compliance with “no net loss” provisions of State law.
12/31/2023
6th Cycle
Continuous
a) The City provides an annual report to the City Council on housing 
production as part of its approval of the Annual Progress Report. 
The City continues to enforce the provisions of No Net Loss law through 
the planning application process. Projects are required to propose a net 
increase of units through redevelopment and provide replacement of any 
deed restricted low-income units.
Meetings
3
This objective is completed on an ongoing 
basis throughout the 2023-2031 period. The 
City Council has reviewed the annual APR for 
2023, 2024, and 2025 and the City has 
submitted the APR to HCD annually. The 
implementation plan dashboard can be 
viewed here: 
https://strivesanmateo.org/housing-element-
implementation/
H1.1 Monitor Regional 
Housing Needs Allocation 
and Pipeline projects
b)   Conduct a mid-cycle review of realistic capacity projections and pipeline project 
housing production to ensure outcomes are aligned with projections. Update sites 
inventory to include additional sites to ensure ongoing compliance with “no net loss” 
provisions of State law.
12/31/2027
6th Cycle
Not Yet Started
b) By 2027, additional sites for housing redevelopment will be identified, 
as necessary, to ensure that the City meets its share of RHNA 
requirements.
Meetings
Not yet started

and Properties for New

areas (aka "Boomerang Funds”), as well as the Affordable Housing Commercial Linkage 
Fee Fund to use for affordable housing development, prioritizing funding allocation to 
projects that include units for extremely low income (ELI) households. 
12/31/2023
6th Cycle
Continuous
a) The City continues to set aside 20% of general fund property tax 
revenues from former RDA areas in a dedicated housing fund to support 
affordable housing production, with a prioritization for ELI households.

basis throughout the 2023-2031 period. City 
operating budget for FY25-26. $1,000,000 
added to the fund.

ter/View/98388/Adopted-2025-26-Budget

H1.4 Support Increased 
Accessory Dwelling Unit 
Development
a)   Amend the City’s ADU Ordinance (SMMC Chapter 27.19) to align with current State 
law and to resolve any inconsistencies identified by HCD.
12/31/2024
6th Cycle
In Progress
a) On October 18, 2024 the City of San Mateo received a letter from HCD 
that detailed portions of the City's ADU Ordinance that it found to be 
inconsistent with state law. On November 15, 2024, the City sent a letter 
to HCD that responded to each of HCD's comments. On August 26, 2025, 
a Planning Commission study session was held to introduce ADU 
Ordinance updates and receive community and Commission feedback. 
The Planning Commission provided staff with feedback on specific local 
changes, outside of those required for State law compliance, in order to 
address community needs.Staff conducted outreach and hosted a virtual 
workshop on local ADU needs and requirements on September 25, 2025. 
On December 9, 2025, the Planning Commission held a public hearing to 
review the draft ADU Ordinance amendments and recommended City 
Council adoption. It is anticipated that the City Council will approve the 
ADU Ordinance in 2026.
Other
The City Council is anticipated to consider the 
ADU ordinance in 2026.
H1.4 Support Increased 
Accessory Dwelling Unit 
Development
b)   Maintain and expand the ADU information and resources available to home owners 
and applicants provided on the City’s website. 
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
Continuous
b) The City maintains an ADU resource website that provides current 
handouts and guidance on the City of San Mateo ADU regulations. The 
City's website is supplemented with the San Mateo County ADU 
Resource Center that the City sponsors.
Other
This objective is completed on an ongoing 
basis throughout the 2023-2031 period. 
Extensive ADU information can be found on 
the City's website here: 
https://www.cityofsanmateo.org/3907
The City also sponsors the San Mateo County 
ADU Resource Center website that has 
additional information here: 
https://smcadu.org/
H1.4 Support Increased 
Accessory Dwelling Unit 
Development
c)   Collaborate with 21 Elements and other jurisdictions in San Mateo County to find 
ways to enhance available ADU resources, establish uniform standards and 
requirements, and effective ways to monitor ADU affordability. 
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
Continuous
c) Through 21 Elements, the City has joined with a County-wide effort to 
establish the San Mateo ADU Resource Center that launched in 2024 to 
support additional ADU construction and monitor affordability. Through 
this collaboration, 21 Elements worked with ABAG to establish a new 
ADU Rental Survey which was initiated in 2025. Also, the ADU Resource 
Center has scheduled ADU informational webinars/workshops for 
residents. The ADU Resource Center will also be hosting a plans gallery 
for compliance with AB 1332. 
Other
This objective is completed on an ongoing 
basis throughout the 2023-2031 period. The 
San Mateo County ADU Resource Center 
webiste can be found here: 
https://smcadu.org/
H1.4 Support Increased 
Accessory Dwelling Unit 
Development
d)   Monitor ADU occupancy and rent levels to verify that affordability allocations. Tools 
to monitor will include, but are not limited to, collaboration with 21 Elements or ABAG to 
develop a uniform approach to tracking ADU affordability countywide, and surveying 
ADU applicants during the building permit review process to understand intended use, 
occupancy and rent charged.
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
Continuous
d) The City worked through the collaboration with 21 Elements and 
ABAG to establish an ADU Rent Survey. The rent survey has been 
approved by HCD to use to monitor rent levels to verify affordability 
allocations. The City has initiated using the survey in 2025 and requires 
property owners to complete the survey prior to issuance of the building 
permit.
Other
This objective is completed on an ongoing 
basis throughout the 2023-2031 period. 
Accessory Dwelling Unit (ADU) Affordability 
Survey Tool:  https://abag.ca.gov/technical-
assistance/accessory-dwelling-unit-adu-
affordability-survey-tool
H1.4 Support Increased 
Accessory Dwelling Unit 
Development
e)   Continue to offer flat fees for ADU building permits, and review ADU permit fees 
every 2-3 years. If fees are found to be a barrier to ADU production, the City will make 
reductions as necessary to reduce cost barriers.  
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
Continuous
The City currently offers flat fees for ADU building permits at a rate that 
is comparable with other jurisdictions in San Mateo County.
Other
This objective is completed on an ongoing 
basis throughout the 2023-2031 period. See

H1.6 Streamline Housing 
Application Review
a)   Update the City Council resolutions that establish the pre-application and 
development review processes to eliminate the pre-application requirement for housing 
projects and eliminate the third-party design review requirement for housing projects 
that meet objective standards. 
12/31/2024
6th Cycle
Completed
a) On July 15, 2024 the San Mateo City Council adopted Resolution No 
65 (2024), repealing Resolution No. 69 (2020) that required a pre-
application process prior to formal applications for housing projects 
greater than six units. Resolution No 65 (2024) adopts a new streamlined 
process for early public engagement. The Council also adopted 
Resolution No. 66 (2024) repealing Resolution No. 67 (1996) that required 
third-party design review of a project. Resolution No.66  (2024) allows 
the Zoning Administrator or the Planning Commission to request a third-
party design review of a proposed housing project if the project is not in 
compliance with the Objective Design Standards.
d) By 2025, the City will adopt code amendments that are consistent with 
SB 330.

H1.6 Streamline Housing 
Application Review
d)   Develop Zoning Code amendments to align City requirements with SB 330 and make 
a recommendation to the City Council.
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
Completed
On July 28, 2025, the City Council adopted  Ordinance 2025-05 that 
amended and added several sections of the Zoning Code to reduce the 
development review timeline for housing projects by streamlining the 
development review process, aligning City requirements with SB 330, 
and adding certainty in the development application process. The 
ordinance established an appeals process when a planning application 
is determined to be ‘incomplete’ subject to the review and determination 
of the Planning Commission.

H1.7 Update Zoning Code 
Standards to Support 
Housing Production
a)   Evaluate the standards and requirements in all zone districts that allow for multi-
family residential and amend the Zoning Code as needed to reduce or eliminate 
constraints to housing construction.  Specifically: 1) exclude covered parking and other 
elements as needed from Floor Area Ratio (FAR) calculation for housing projects with 
affordable units, 2) specify where Single-Room Occupancy (SRO) units are permitted 
consistent with government Code 65583(c)(1), and 3) specify that Manufactured Homes 
that are built on a permanent foundation are subject to the same zoning requirements 
and development standards as single-family homes.
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
Completed
1). The Land Use Element of the General Plan 2040 does not have a FAR 
limitation/maximum for housing development projects. Any Zoning 
Districts standards that are conflict are superseded with the General 
Plan policies; therefore, FAR maximums are not being applied to 
housing development projects. Comprehensive zoning code 
amendments are planned in 2026 to remove zoning code conflicts with 
General Plan policies.
2) and 3) - On April 7, 2025, the City Council adopted an ordinance to 
update the zoning code to establish a definition for Single -Room 
Occupancy units and allow as a permitted use in R4 zoning Districts. 
The ordinance aslo added a provision that Manufactured Homes that are 
built on a permanent foundation are subject to the same zoning 
requirements and development standards as single-family homes.
Meetings
1
Ordinance No. 2025-03.: 
https://law.cityofsanmateo.org/us/ca/cities/san-
mateo/ordinances/2025/3
H1.7 Update Zoning Code 
Standards to Support 
Housing Production
b)   Amend the Zoning Code to reduce off-street parking requirements for residential 
units (SMMC Chapter 27.64) as follows:  Studio and 1 Bedroom units to 1 space, 2 
Bedrooms and above to 1.5 spaces; and for projects within ½ mile of transit, consistent 
with AB2097, no off-street parking shall be required. As part of this amendment, staff 
will review all other parking related requirements and make recommendations to reduce 
or eliminate any other parking related requirements identified as a constraint. 
12/31/2025
6th Cycle
In Progress
b) In 2024, the City initiated work to explore parking requiring including 
amendments to the Zoning Code to reduce off-street parking 
requirements for residential uses. In 2025, the City has been working 
through an MTC TOC Technical Assistance Grant through the 
Metropolitan Transportation Commission to identify parking policy and 
standard reforms that align with state law and regional efforts. It is 
anticipated that this work will be concluded in 2026 and the work efforts 
will be folded into the Comprehensive Zoning Code Update.
Meetings
None

H1.9 Establish Minimum 
Densities for Mixed-Use 
Projects
a)   Amend the Zoning Code to require that properties designated for mixed-uses, in the 
Sites Inventory List, allow 100 percent residential use and require that residential use 
occupy 50 percent or more of the total floor area of a mixed-use project consistent with 
Government Code Section 65583(h), and establish a minimum residential density for 
mixed-use projects elsewhere in the City that include a residential component.
12/31/2024
6th Cycle
Completed
a) In 2024, the City initiated the comprehensive code amendments to 
permit 100 percent residential uses on properties zoned for mixed uses. 
These Zoning Code amendments will be introduced to the Council for 
adoption in 2025 aggregated with other code amendments. The 
reference to Government Code Section 65583(h) is incorrectly stated. 
The correct reference should be Government Code Section 65583.2, 
subdivisions (h) and (i). Compliance is only required when the sites 
inventory does not identify site to accommodate the need for groups of 
all household income levels. However, the San Mateo Housing Element 
sites inventory did accomodate sites for all of its household income 
levels without the need for rezonings; therefore, nothing further is 
necessary to comply with the Government Code. In 2023, the City 
initiated policy work with a consultant to evaluate minimum residential 
density requirements for large commercial mixed-use projects. On 
August 19, 2024, the City Council held a study session on requiring a 
minimum residential density in mixed-use projects with a residential 
component. On July 28th, 2025, the City Council adopted an ordinance 
that establishes minimum residential densities for mixed-use projects 
that include a residential component with five or more units.
Meetings
1
Ordinance 2025-05: 
https://law.cityofsanmateo.org/us/ca/cities/san-
mateo/ordinances/2025/5
H1.9 Establish Minimum 
Densities for Mixed-Use 
Projects
b)   Study and present to City Council options for requiring a residential component in 
all mixed-use projects in certain zones or areas. 
12/31/2024
6th Cycle
Completed
b) At a City Council Study Session on August 19, 2024, city staff 
provided an overview of requiring minimum residential densities and 
requiring a residential component in all mixed-use projects. The Council 
reached consensus to further explore setting minimum residential 
density requirements for mixed-use
developments, but they did not reach consensus on requiring all mixed-
use projects to require residential units.
